1	"""Naive W4A16 weight-only quantized GEMM reference (correctness only).
2	
3	AWQ/GPTQ-style scheme:
4	  x:      (M, K)               bf16
5	  w_q:    (K // 2, N)          uint8   -- two int4 weights packed per byte (low nibble = even-K, high = odd-K)
6	  scales: (K // group, N)      bf16
7	  zeros:  (K // group, N)      bf16    -- asymmetric (stored already as float zero-point)
8	  out:    (M, N)                bf16
9	
10	Dequant (per group along K):
11	  w_bf[k, n] = (w_q[k, n] - zeros[k // group, n]) * scales[k // group, n]
12	where w_q[k, n] is the unpacked 4-bit value (0..15).
13	
14	This reference unpacks to a full bf16 matrix and then runs torch.matmul. Slow and
15	memory-heavy on the dequant; the agent's solution must fuse unpack+GEMM.
16	"""
17	from __future__ import annotations
18	
19	import torch
20	import torch.nn as nn
21	
22	OP_TYPE = "gemm_w4a16"
23	SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["int4_bf16"]
24	HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
25	
26	GROUP_SIZE = 128
27	
28	
29	def _pack_int4(w_q: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
30	    """Pack (K, N) uint8 in [0,15] into (K//2, N) uint8.
31	
32	    Even rows go in the low nibble, odd rows in the high nibble.
33	    """
34	    K, N = w_q.shape
35	    assert K % 2 == 0
36	    lo = w_q[0::2].to(torch.uint8) & 0xF
37	    hi = w_q[1::2].to(torch.uint8) & 0xF
38	    return (lo | (hi << 4)).contiguous()
39	
40	
41	def _unpack_int4(w_packed: torch.Tensor, K: int) -> torch.Tensor:
42	    """Unpack (K//2, N) uint8 -> (K, N)

1	I need you to write a weight-only int4 quantized GEMM (W4A16) for the RTX PRO 6000 (SM120 Blackwell, GDDR7, 1.8 TB/s). The reference implementation is in reference.py and your kernel goes in solution.py. You can make whatever mess you want in this directory — scratch files, intermediate kernels, build artifacts, profiling traces — but the final answer has to be in solution.py with the same Model, get_inputs, and get_init_inputs interface as reference.py.
2	
3	The scheme is AWQ/GPTQ-style asymmetric int4 with explicit zero-points and per-group bf16 scales. Inputs are x of shape (M, K) bf16, w_q of shape (K // 2, N) uint8 (two int4 weights packed per byte, low nibble = even-K row, high nibble = odd-K row), scales of shape (K // 128, N) bf16, and zeros of shape (K // 128, N) bf16. Group size is 128 along K. Dequant per group is w_bf[k, n] = (unpack(w_q)[k, n] - zeros[k // 128, n]) * scales[k // 128, n], and the output is (M, N) bf16. Correctness tolerance is 0.10 abs/rel — group-quant adds noise on top of bf16 accumulator slop. The shapes you have to handle are M=1 N=12288 K=4096 (decode, memory-bound on the int4 weight read), M=32 N=12288 K=4096 (small prefill, mixed regime), M=256 N=12288 K=4096 (larger prefill, approaching compute-bound), M=1 N=4096 K=4096 (decode, square), and M=16 N=14336 K=4096 (speculative-decode-ish).
4	
5	This needs to be a real custom kernel that fuses unpack and GEMM in the same pass — a separate dequant-then-matmul wastes the entire bandwidth advan

reasoning (18353 chars)
Let me understand the problem comprehensively: 1. **Goal**: Write a fused W4A16 (weight-only int4 quantized) GEMM kernel for RTX PRO 6000 (Blackwell SM120). 2. **Scheme**: AWQ/GPTQ-style asymmetric int4 with explicit zero-points and per-group bf16 scales. 3. **Group size**: 128 along K dimension. 4. **Shapes**: Various decode/prefill shapes (M=1 to M=256). 5. **Requirements**: Must fuse unpack + GEMM, not just dequant then matmul. 6. **Forbidden**: bitsandbytes.functional.dequantize_4bit, gemv_4bit, marlin_kernel.gemm, torch.nn.functional.linear. Let me think about what approach to take: **RTX PRO 6000 = Blackwell SM120 = compute capability 12.0** Options: 1. **Triton kernel**: Write a Triton kernel with fused dequant. Triton supports Blackwell now. This is probably the most practical approach for a custom kernel. 2. **CUDA C++ via load_inline**: The most flexible, but requires deep CUDA knowledge. 3. **Inline PTX**: Very low-level, hard to get right. Given the constraints and the need for performance, I'll try a **Triton kernel** approach first. Triton can fuse the dequantization into the compute, and with proper tuning can approach close to memory bandwidth limits. Key performance considerations: - For M=1 (decode), this is memory-bound on the weight read. The packed int4 weights are (K/2 * N) bytes = 4096/2 * 12288 = ~25 MB. At 1.8 TB/s, the theoretical lower bound is ~14 µs. Realistically, we need to saturate the memory bandwidth. - For larger M (prefill), it becomes more compute-bound. The Triton approach: - Write a fused kernel that loads packed int4 weights, unpacks them, dequantizes with scales/zeros, and accumulates into the output. - Use block-level parallelism: tile over M (batch) and N (output columns). - K dimension is reduced with block-wise accumulation. Let me think about the Triton kernel design more carefully: For W4A16 GEMM with Triton: - Each program loads a tile of x (M_tile x K_tile) and a tile of packed weights (K_tile/2 x N_tile) - Unpack the weight tile, apply scales/zeros - Accumulate dot product - Output (M_tile x N_tile) But wait — Triton's `tl.dot` expects specific input formats. For Blackwell (SM120), Triton might support different data types. Actually, let me reconsider. The key challenge is that int4 isn't natively supported by Triton's `tl.dot`. We'd need to unpack to a wider type first, then use `tl.dot`. Let me think about what data types Triton supports for dot products: - fp16, bf16, fp32, tf32, int8, etc. - Not int4 directly. So the approach would be: 1. Load packed uint8 weights 2. Unpack to int8 or bf16 3. Apply dequant (subtract zeros, multiply by scales) to get bf16 4. Use tl.dot with bf16 inputs But this means we're doing the same amount of compute as a bf16 matmul, just with less memory traffic. The key value is the bandwidth savings from reading int4 weights instead of bf16 weights. Wait, actually there's a subtlety. If we dequant to bf16 and then do tl.dot, we're paying the full compute cost of bf16 matmul. But we save 4x on weight memory bandwidth. For decode (M=1, memory-bound), this is a big win. For larger M, the compute savings would matter more. Actually, for really fusing it, we could try to use integer dot product instructions if available on Blackwell. But that gets complex. Let me think about what would actually be fast: **Approach 1: Triton kernel with fused dequant to bf16, then tl.dot** - Loads int4 weights, unpacks to bf16 (fused dequant), does tl.dot with bf16 - Simple, clean, should work - Memory-bound savings from 4x less weight data - For compute-bound shapes, this does bf16 compute, which is fine on Blackwell (it has lots of bf16 throughput) **Approach 2: CUDA C++ with tensor core int4 instructions** - Blackwell (SM120) might have special int4 tensor core instructions - This would be faster but much harder to implement **Approach 3: PTX with mma instructions** - Direct tensor core programming - Very complex Let me start with **Triton** as it's the most practical and well-supported on Blackwell. Triton has been actively supporting Blackwell GPUs. Let me write a Triton kernel that: 1. Takes x (M, K) bf16, w_q (K//2, N) uint8, scales (K//128, N) bf16, zeros (K//128, N) bf16 2. For each program (pid_m, pid_n): - Load tile of x: (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) bf16 - Load tile of w_q: (BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N) uint8 - Unpack to (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) - Broadcast scales/zeros from group level - Dequant: (w_unpacked - zeros) * scales → bf16 - Accumulate with tl.dot Wait, there's a complexity. The group structure means scales/zeros are per group of 128 along K. Within a BLOCK_K tile (which crosses multiple groups), we need different scales/zeros for different K positions. Let me think about this more carefully. The packed weight layout: - w_q shape: (K//2, N) uint8 - Low nibble = even K rows, high nibble = odd K rows - scales shape: (K//128, N) bf16 - zeros shape: (K//128, N) bf16 For a given weight element at (k, n): - group_id = k // 128 - w_deq = (unpack(w_q[k, n]) - zeros[group_id, n]) * scales[group_id, n] In Triton, when I load a tile of w_q at positions [k_start:k_start+BLOCK_K, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N], I need to know the group for each k position. Let me plan the kernel: ```python @triton.jit def gemm_w4a16_kernel( x_ptr, w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, out_ptr, M, N, K, stride_xm, stride_xk, stride_wq_kh, stride_wq_n, stride_s_g, stride_s_n, stride_z_g, stride_z_n, stride_om, stride_on, GROUP_SIZE_M: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_SIZE_M: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_SIZE_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_SIZE_K: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) num_pid_m = tl.cdiv(M, BLOCK_SIZE_M) num_pid_n = tl.cdiv(N, BLOCK_SIZE_N) pid_m = pid // num_pid_n pid_n = pid % num_pid_n # Ranges for this block offs_m = pid_m * BLOCK_SIZE_M + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE_M) offs_n = pid_n * BLOCK_SIZE_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE_N) offs_k = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE_K) # Masks mask_m = offs_m < M mask_n = offs_n < N # Accumulator acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_SIZE_M, BLOCK_SIZE_N), dtype=tl.float32) for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_SIZE_K): k_offs = k_start + offs_k mask_k = k_offs < K # Load x tile: (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) x_ptrs = x_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_xm + k_offs[None, :] * stride_xk x = tl.load(x_ptrs, mask=mask_m[:, None] & mask_k[None, :], other=0.0) # Load packed weights: (BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N) # Each byte contains 2 int4 weights k_half = k_offs // 2 # indices into the packed dimension wq_ptrs = w_q_ptr + k_half[:, None] * stride_wq_kh + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wq_n # ... this gets tricky because we need to handle even/odd K # Actually, let me reconsider the approach. ``` Hmm, this is getting complex. Let me think about a cleaner approach. Actually, for Triton, a cleaner approach might be: 1. Load a block of packed weights 2. Unpack them in registers 3. Apply dequant 4. Do tl.dot For the unpacking, I need to handle the nibble extraction within Triton. Let me think about what's actually efficient. In Triton, `tl.load` is the fundamental operation. I can load uint8 data. Then I need to extract low and high nibbles. ```python # Load packed as uint8, then extract nibbles w_packed = tl.load(wq_ptrs, mask=..., other=0) # shape (BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N), uint8 w_lo = w_packed & 0x0F # even rows, but these are uint8 w_hi = (w_packed >> 4) & 0x0F # odd rows, but these are uint8 # Now I need to interleave them: w_unpacked[k, n] = w_lo if k even else w_hi # But the structure is: w_packed[k//2, n] gives lo at even k, hi at odd k # So for k in 0..BLOCK_K-1: # if k % 2 == 0: w_unpacked[k, n] = w_packed[k//2, n] & 0xF # else: w_unpacked[k, n] = w_packed[k//2, n] >> 4 ``` This interleaving is a bit annoying in Triton. Let me think... Actually, I can use tl.view or reshaping. Or I can just handle even and odd rows separately in the accumulation. Wait, let me think about this differently. The key insight is that each byte in w_q gives us two int4 values. If I'm doing a dot product, I can process even and odd K rows separately: ```python for k_half in range(0, K//2, BLOCK_SIZE_K//2): # Load even rows of X (indices 2*k_half, 2*k_half+2, ...) # Load odd rows of X (indices 2*k_half+1, 2*k_half+3, ...) # Load w_packed (BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N) # w_lo for even rows, w_hi for odd rows # Dequant separately # Accumulate ``` Actually, I think the cleanest approach is to restructure the unpacking in Triton. Let me use a 2-step approach in the K loop: For each K block: 1. Load w_packed[BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N] as uint8 2. Extract low nibbles (even K) into w_even[BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N] 3. Extract high nibbles (odd K) into w_odd[BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N] 4. Load x_even and x_odd 5. Compute group indices for even and odd K positions 6. Load scales/zeros for those groups 7. Dequant: w_even_deq = (w_even - zero_even) * scale_even, w_odd_deq = (w_odd - zero_odd) * scale_odd 8. acc += tl.dot(x_even, w_even_deq) + tl.dot(x_odd, w_odd_deq) This gets complex with the group structure (group_size=128 along K). Each BLOCK_K//2 even-row group might span multiple quantization groups. Hmm, let me take a step back and think about what makes this fast. For the memory-bound case (M=1, decode), the bottleneck is reading the weights. The key optimization is: 1. Read packed int4 weights (4x less data than bf16) 2. Unpack and dequant on the fly in registers 3. Keep the accumulator in registers The compute pattern is essentially a vector-matrix product for M=1, or a small matrix-matrix product for larger M. Actually, let me think about what Triton does under the hood. `tl.dot` on Blackwell uses tensor cores (mma instructions). The inputs to mma need to be in specific formats. For bf16, the inputs are bf16 and the accumulator is f32. So the plan is: 1. Load packed weights as uint8 2. Unpack, dequant to bf16 3. Use tl.dot with bf16 x and bf16 dequantized weights 4. Store bf16 output This should work and be reasonably fast. The key bandwidth savings come from step 1 (reading 4x less weight data). Let me now write the actual kernel. Here's my plan: For each K block of size BLOCK_K: - I need to handle BLOCK_K//2 packed bytes, each giving 2 weight values - The even K positions (0, 2, 4, ..., BLOCK_K-2) come from low nibbles - The odd K positions (1, 3, 5, ..., BLOCK_K-1) come from high nibbles I can load the packed bytes once, extract both nibbles, and then do two separate dot products (one for even K positions, one for odd K positions). Actually, you know what, let me think about this even more carefully. In the Triton matmul tutorial, they use `tl.dot` which expects the K dimension to be the inner dimension of both matrices. If I have: - x: (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) bf16 - w_deq: (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) bf16 Then `tl.dot(x, w_deq)` gives (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N) f32. The complexity is that w_deq isn't stored contiguously — I need to construct it from packed uint8 + scales + zeros. Let me just construct the dequantized weight tile in Triton. ```python # For each k in the block, group_id = k_abs // 128 # For a given (k_abs, n) position: # w_byte = w_q[k_abs // 2, n] # w_int4 = (w_byte >> (4 * (k_abs % 2))) & 0xF # low nibble for even k, high for odd k # w_deq = (float(w_int4) - zeros[k_abs // 128, n]) * scales[k_abs // 128, n] ``` In Triton, I'd need to: 1. Load w_packed for the K block and N block 2. Extract even and odd nibbles 3. Load scales/zeros for the groups spanned by this K block 4. Broadcast scales/zeros to the K dimension 5. Compute w_deq as bf16 6. Do tl.dot Let me implement this step by step. For step 2-5, a key efficiency consideration: within a BLOCK_K=128 block, there are at most 2 groups (if BLOCK_K=128, exactly 1 group fits, but if BLOCK_K > 128, multiple groups). Let me pick BLOCK_K=128 to keep things simple — then each K block corresponds to exactly one group, and scales/zeros are constant within the block. Wait, but BLOCK_K=128 means I'm loading 64 packed bytes per N column, which is 64*BLOCK_N bytes. That's a lot of data per iteration. Let me think about what block sizes make sense. Then each K tile is exactly one group, and scales/zeros are constant within the tile. This makes the dequant very clean. For the decode case (M=1, N=12288, K=4096): - Int4 weight data: K/2 * N = 2048 * 12288 = ~25 MB - Scales: K/128 * N * 2 = 32 * 12288 * 2 = ~786 KB - Zeros: same = ~786 KB - X input: M * K * 2 = 1 * 4096 * 2 = ~8 KB - Output: M * N * 2 = 1 * 12288 * 2 = ~24 KB Total memory traffic ≈ ~26.6 MB per call. At 1.8 TB/s, theoretical minimum time = 26.6 MB / 1.8 TB/s ≈ 14.8 µs Dense FLOPs: 2 * 1 * 12288 * 4096 = ~100 MFLOPs = 0.1 GFLOPs So this is purely bandwidth-bound. The goal is to get as close to memory bandwidth as possible. 2 * M * N * K FLOPs would be 2 * 1 * 12288 * 4096 = 100,663,296 FLOPs ≈ 0.1 GFLOPS. At 1.8 TB/s bandwidth, we can do at most 1.8e12 / 26.6e6 ≈ 67,669 calls/second, or 14.8 µs per call. import torch
import triton
import triton.language as tl

@triton.jit
def test_uint8_kernel(x_ptr, out_ptr, n: tl.constexpr):
    offs = tl.arange(0, n)
    x = tl.load(x_ptr + offs)
    out = x & 0x0F
    tl.store(out_ptr + offs, out)

x = torch.tensor([0x12, 0x34, 0x56, 0x78], dtype=torch.uint8, device='cuda')
out = torch.empty(4, dtype=torch.uint8, device='cuda')
test_uint8_kernel[(1,)](x, out, n=4)
print("input:", x.tolist())
print("output:", out.tolist())
print("uint8 works!")

"""Test the fused W4A16 GEMM kernel approach."""
import torch
import triton
import triton.language as tl

GROUP_SIZE = 128

@triton.jit
def gemm_w4a16_kernel(
    x_ptr, w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, out_ptr,
    M, N, K,
    stride_xm, stride_xk,
    stride_wq_kh, stride_wq_n,
    stride_s_g, stride_s_n,
    stride_z_g, stride_z_n,
    stride_om, stride_on,
    BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr,
):
    """Fused W4A16 GEMM: unpack + dequant + matmul in one kernel."""
    pid = tl.program_id(0)
    num_pid_m = tl.cdiv(M, BLOCK_M)
    num_pid_n = tl.cdiv(N, BLOCK_N)
    pid_m = pid // num_pid_n
    pid_n = pid % num_pid_n

    offs_m = pid_m * BLOCK_M + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M)
    offs_n = pid_n * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)

    mask_m = offs_m < M
    mask_n = offs_n < N

    acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)

    for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K):
        # --- Load packed weights ---
        k_half_base = k_start // 2
        k_half_offs = k_half_base + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2)
        wq_ptrs = w_q_ptr + k_half_offs[:, None] * stride_wq_kh + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wq_n
        mask_wq = (k_half_offs[:, None] < K // 2) & mask_n[None, :]
        w_packed = tl.load(wq_ptrs, mask=mask_wq, other=0)
        # w_packed: (BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N) uint8

        # --- Unpack nibbles ---
        w_lo = w_packed & 0x0F   # even K rows: (BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N) uint8
        w_hi = (w_packed >> 4) & 0x0F  # odd K rows: (BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N) uint8

        # --- Load scales and zeros for this group ---
        group_id = k_start // GROUP_SIZE
        s_ptrs = scales_ptr + group_id * stride_s_g + offs_n * stride_s_n
        z_ptrs = zeros_ptr + group_id * stride_z_g + offs_n * stride_z_n
        scales_g = tl.load(s_ptrs, mask=mask_n, other=0.0)  # (BLOCK_N,)
        zeros_g = tl.load(z_ptrs, mask=mask_n, other=0.0)   # (BLOCK_N,)

        # --- Dequant: (w_int - zero) * scale ---
        w_lo_f32 = w_lo.to(tl.float32) - zeros_g[None, :].to(tl.float32)
        w_lo_f32 = w_lo_f32 * scales_g[None, :].to(tl.float32)
        w_lo_bf16 = w_lo_f32.to(tl.bfloat16)  # (BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N)

        w_hi_f32 = w_hi.to(tl.float32) - zeros_g[None, :].to(tl.float32)
        w_hi_f32 = w_hi_f32 * scales_g[None, :].to(tl.float32)
        w_hi_bf16 = w_hi_f32.to(tl.bfloat16)  # (BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N)

        # --- Load x (activations) for even and odd K rows ---
        k_even_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) * 2
        k_odd_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) * 2 + 1

        x_even_ptrs = x_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_xm + k_even_offs[None, :] * stride_xk
        x_odd_ptrs = x_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_xm + k_odd_offs[None, :] * stride_xk

        mask_xe = mask_m[:, None] & (k_even_offs[None, :] < K)
        mask_xo = mask_m[:, None] & (k_odd_offs[None, :] < K)

        x_even = tl.load(x_even_ptrs, mask=mask_xe, other=0.0)  # (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K//2)
        x_odd = tl.load(x_odd_ptrs, mask=mask_xo, other=0.0)    # (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K//2)

        # --- Accumulate ---
        acc += tl.dot(x_even, w_lo_bf16)  # (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K//2) @ (BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N)
        acc += tl.dot(x_odd, w_hi_bf16)

    # --- Store output ---
    out_ptrs = out_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_om + offs_n[None, :] * stride_on
    tl.store(out_ptrs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_m[:, None] & mask_n[None, :])


def test_small():
    """Test with a small shape."""
    M, N, K = 2, 64, 256
    torch.manual_seed(42)

    # Create synthetic quantized weights
    n_groups = K // GROUP_SIZE
    w_full = torch.randn(K, N, dtype=torch.float32) * 0.1
    w_g = w_full.view(n_groups, GROUP_SIZE, N)
    w_min = w_g.min(dim=1, keepdim=True).values
    w_max = w_g.max(dim=1, keepdim=True).values
    scales = (w_max - w_min).clamp_min(1e-8) / 15.0
    zeros = (-w_min / scales).round().clamp(0, 15)
    
    w_q = ((w_g / scales) + zeros).round().clamp(0, 15).to(torch.uint8)

"""Test the fused W4A16 GEMM kernel approach."""
import torch
import triton
import triton.language as tl

@triton.jit
def gemm_w4a16_kernel(
    x_ptr, w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, out_ptr,
    M, N, K,
    stride_xm, stride_xk,
    stride_wq_kh, stride_wq_n,
    stride_s_g, stride_s_n,
    stride_z_g, stride_z_n,
    stride_om, stride_on,
    BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr,
    GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr,
):
    """Fused W4A16 GEMM: unpack + dequant + matmul in one kernel."""
    pid = tl.program_id(0)
    num_pid_m = tl.cdiv(M, BLOCK_M)
    num_pid_n = tl.cdiv(N, BLOCK_N)
    pid_m = pid // num_pid_n
    pid_n = pid % num_pid_n

    offs_m = pid_m * BLOCK_M + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M)
    offs_n = pid_n * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)

    mask_m = offs_m < M
    mask_n = offs_n < N

    acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)

    for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K):
        # --- Load packed weights ---
        k_half_base = k_start // 2
        k_half_offs = k_half_base + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2)
        wq_ptrs = w_q_ptr + k_half_offs[:, None] * stride_wq_kh + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wq_n
        mask_wq = (k_half_offs[:, None] < K // 2) & mask_n[None, :]
        w_packed = tl.load(wq_ptrs, mask=mask_wq, other=0)

        # --- Unpack nibbles ---
        w_lo = w_packed & 0x0F
        w_hi = (w_packed >> 4) & 0x0F

        # --- Load scales and zeros for this group ---
        group_id = k_start // GROUP_SIZE
        s_ptrs = scales_ptr + group_id * stride_s_g + offs_n * stride_s_n
        z_ptrs = zeros_ptr + group_id * stride_z_g + offs_n * stride_z_n
        scales_g = tl.load(s_ptrs, mask=mask_n, other=0.0)
        zeros_g = tl.load(z_ptrs, mask=mask_n, other=0.0)

        # --- Dequant: (w_int - zero) * scale ---
        w_lo_f32 = w_lo.to(tl.float32) - zeros_g[None, :].to(tl.float32)
        w_lo_f32 = w_lo_f32 * scales_g[None, :].to(tl.float32)
        w_lo_bf16 = w_lo_f32.to(tl.bfloat16)

        w_hi_f32 = w_hi.to(tl.float32) - zeros_g[None, :].to(tl.float32)
        w_hi_f32 = w_hi_f32 * scales_g[None, :].to(tl.float32)
        w_hi_bf16 = w_hi_f32.to(tl.bfloat16)

        # --- Load x for even and odd K rows ---
        k_even_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) * 2
        k_odd_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) * 2 + 1

        x_even_ptrs = x_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_xm + k_even_offs[None, :] * stride_xk
        x_odd_ptrs = x_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_xm + k_odd_offs[None, :] * stride_xk

        mask_xe = mask_m[:, None] & (k_even_offs[None, :] < K)
        mask_xo = mask_m[:, None] & (k_odd_offs[None, :] < K)

        x_even = tl.load(x_even_ptrs, mask=mask_xe, other=0.0)
        x_odd = tl.load(x_odd_ptrs, mask=mask_xo, other=0.0)

        # --- Accumulate ---
        acc += tl.dot(x_even, w_lo_bf16)
        acc += tl.dot(x_odd, w_hi_bf16)

    # --- Store output ---
    out_ptrs = out_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_om + offs_n[None, :] * stride_on
    tl.store(out_ptrs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_m[:, None] & mask_n[None, :])

One potential issue: the w_q tensor is uint8, but Triton might expect specific alignment. Let me make sure the tensors are contiguous before passing to the kernel. OK, let me write solution.py now. Actually, I also want to think about whether I should handle the case where K is not divisible by GROUP_SIZE. In the shapes we have, K=4096 and GROUP_SIZE=128, so 4096/128 = 32, which is fine. But for robustness, let me handle it. Also, BLOCK_K must evenly divide GROUP_SIZE for the dequant to work correctly within a single K block. With GROUP_SIZE=128, valid BLOCK_K values are: 16, 32, 64, 128. If BLOCK_K=128, each K block is exactly one group. If BLOCK_K=64, two K blocks share one group. If BLOCK_K=32, four K blocks share one group, etc. Actually, wait. BLOCK_K doesn't have to be a divisor of GROUP_SIZE! The issue is that within a K block, I compute `group_id = k_start // GROUP_SIZE`, which works regardless. The scale/zero are loaded once per K block, and if the K block spans multiple groups, the scale/zero would be wrong for some elements. Wait no. Let me think again. The `k_start` loop iterates in steps of BLOCK_K. Within the loop body, `group_id = k_start // GROUP_SIZE` gives us the group for the first element of the block. If BLOCK_K <= GROUP_SIZE and k_start is always a multiple of BLOCK_K, then the block can't span a group boundary unless k_start is not aligned to GROUP_SIZE. For correctness, I need BLOCK_K to be a divisor of GROUP_SIZE, so that k_start is always at a group boundary. If BLOCK_K=128 and GROUP_SIZE=128, then k_start is always 0, 128, 256, ... and each block is exactly one group — clean. If BLOCK_K=64 and GROUP_SIZE=128, then k_start alternates between even and odd group offsets. But both 0*64 and 1*64 are within group 0, and 2*64=128 is in group 1. Wait, 1*64=64 is in group 0 (since 64//128=0), and 2*64=128 is in group 1. So both k_start=0 and k_start=64 are in group 0 — but they load DIFFERENT packed weight elements! The k_start=0 block loads elements 0-63, and the k_start=64 block loads elements 64-127. Both are in group 0. The group_id computation is correct. But the issue is when k_start is NOT aligned to GROUP_SIZE. Say BLOCK_K=96 and GROUP_SIZE=128: - k_start=0: loads elements 0-95, all in group 0 (correct) - k_start=96: loads elements 96-191. Elements 96-127 are in group 0, but elements 128-191 are in group 1! The scale/zero from group 0 (96//128=0) would be applied to elements 128-191, which is WRONG. So the requirement is: each K block must be fully contained within a single group. This means either: 1. BLOCK_K divides GROUP_SIZE (so k_start is always a group boundary) 2. Or we handle multiple groups within a K block (more complex) I'll go with option 1: BLOCK_K must divide GROUP_SIZE. The valid values for GROUP_SIZE=128 are: 128, 64, 32, 16, 8, 4, 2, 1. For performance, 128, 64, and 32 are the most reasonable choices. Let me support all three in autotuning. +"""W4A16 weight-only quantized GEMM — fused unpack + dequant + matmul via Triton.
+
+AWQ/GPTQ-style asymmetric int4 scheme with explicit zero-points and per-group
+(group_size=128) bf16 scales. The Triton kernel loads packed uint8 weights,
+unpacks nibbles, applies the per-group affine dequant, and accumulates with
+bf16 activations in a single pass — no materialised bf16 weight matrix.
+"""
+
+from __future__ import annotations
+
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+
+OP_TYPE = "gemm_w4a16"
+SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["int4_bf16"]
+HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
+
+GROUP_SIZE = 128
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# Triton kernel
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+@triton.jit
+def _gemm_w4a16_kernel(
+    x_ptr,
+    w_q_ptr,
+    scales_ptr,
+    zeros_ptr,
+    out_ptr,
+    M,
+    N,
+    K,
+    stride_xm,
+    stride_xk,
+    stride_wq_kh,
+    stride_wq_n,
+    stride_s_g,
+    stride_s_n,
+    stride_z_g,
+    stride_z_n,
+    stride_om,
+    stride_on,
+    BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr,
+    BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr,
+    BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr,
+    GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr,
+    GROUP_M: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    """Fused W4A16 GEMM.
+
+    Each program computes a (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N) tile of the output.  The
+    reduction loops over K in steps of BLOCK_K, which must divide GROUP_SIZE
+    so that every K-block sits entirely inside a single quantisation group.
+    """
+    pid = tl.program_id(0)
+    num_pid_m = tl.cdiv(M, BLOCK_M)
+    num_pid_n = tl.cdiv(N, BLOCK_N)
+    num_pid_in_group = GROUP_M * num_pid_n
+    group_id_m = pid // num_pid_in_group
+    first_pid_m = group_id_m * GROUP_M
+    group_size_m = min(num_pid_m - first_pid_m, GROUP_M)
+    pid_m = first_pid_m + (pid % group_size_m)
+    pid_n = (pid % num_pid_in_group) // group_size_m
+
+    offs_m = pid_m * BLOCK_M + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M)
+    offs_n = pid_n * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)
+    mask_m = offs_m < M
+    mask_n = offs_n < N
+
+    # Pointers to the start of the M / N slices
+    x_base = x_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_xm
+    out_base = out_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_om
+
+    acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
+
+    # The kernel assumes BLOCK_K divides GROUP_SIZE so that every K tile sits
+    # inside a single quantisation group.
+    half_k = BLOCK_K // 2
+    k_even_base = tl.arange(0, half_k) * 2       # 0, 2, 4, ..., BLOCK_K-2
+    k_odd_base = k_even_base + 1                  # 1, 3, 5, ..., BLOCK_K-1
+    kh_base = tl.arange(0, half_k)                # packed-byte offsets
+
+    for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K):
+        # --- packed weights for this K tile --------------------------------
+        k_half_offs = (k_start // 2) + kh_base
+        wq_ptrs = w_q_ptr + k_half_offs[:, None] * stride_wq_kh + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wq_n
+        mask_wq = (k_half_offs[:, None] < K // 2) & mask_n[None, :]
+        w_packed = tl.load(wq_ptrs, mask=mask_wq, other=0)
+
+        # Unpack nibbles: low = even K rows, high = odd K rows
+        w_lo = w_packed & 0x0F
+        w_hi = (w_packed >> 4) & 0x0F
+
+        # --- per-group scales & zeros --------------------------------------
+        gid = k_start // GROUP_SIZE
+        s_ptrs = scales_ptr + gid * stride_s_g + offs_n * stride_s_n
+        z_ptrs = zeros_ptr + gid * stride_z_g + offs_n * stride_z_n
+        s_g = tl.load(s_ptrs, mask=mask_n, other=0.0)   # (BLOCK_N,)
+        z_g = tl.load(z_ptrs, mask=mask_n, other=0.0)   # (BLOCK_N,)
+
+        # --- dequant:  w_bf = (w_int4 - zero) * scale  ---------------------
+        s_g_f32 = s_g.to(tl.float32)[None, :]
+        z_g_f32 = z_g.to(tl.float32)[None, :]
+
+        w_lo_bf = ((w_lo.to(tl.float32) - z_g_f32) * s_g_f32).to(tl.bfloat16)
+        w_hi_bf = ((w_hi.to(tl.float32) - z_g_f32) * s_g_f32).to(tl.bfloat16)
+
+        # --- activations (even / odd K rows) -------------------------------
+        k_even = k_start + k_even_base
+        k_odd = k_start + k_odd_base
+
+        x_even_ptrs = x_base + k_even[None, :] * stride_xk
+        x_odd_ptrs = x_base + k_odd[None, :] * stride_xk
+
+        mask_ke = mask_m[:, None] & (k_even[None, :] < K)
+        mask_ko = mask_m[:, None] & (k_odd[None, :] < K)
+
+        x_even = tl.load(x_even_ptrs, mask=mask_ke, other=0.0)
+        x_odd = tl.load(x_odd_ptrs, mask=mask_ko, other=0.0)
+
+        # --- accumulate ----------------------------------------------------
+        acc += tl.dot(x_even, w_lo_bf)
+        acc += tl.dot(x_odd, w_hi_bf)
+
+    # --- store ------------------------------------------------------------
+    out_ptrs = out_base + offs_n[None, :] * stride_on
+    tl.store(out_ptrs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_m[:, None] & mask_n[None, :])
+
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# Autotune configuration
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+_BLOCK_K_OPTIONS = [32, 64, 128]   # must divide GROUP_SIZE (128)
+
+
+def _configs():
+    """Return a list of Triton autotune Config objects.
+
+    We explore a range of tile sizes appropriate for the small-M decode regime
+    (where memory bandwidth matters most) through to larger prefill batches.
+    """
+    configs = []
+    for BLOCK_M in [1, 4, 8, 16, 32, 64]:
+        for BLOCK_N in [32, 64, 128]:
+            for BLOCK_K in _BLOCK_K_OPTIONS:
+                for GROUP_M in [1, 4, 8]:
+                    # Skip absurd combinations
+                    if BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N > 4096:
+                        continue
+                    configs.append(
+                        triton.Config(
+                            {
+                                "BLOCK_M": BLOCK_M,
+                                "BLOCK_N": BLOCK_N,
+             

... (4686 more chars)

"""Quick manual config test to find best block sizes."""
import torch
import triton
import triton.language as tl
import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
from solution import _gemm_w4a16_kernel, GROUP_SIZE
import reference
import shapes

torch.manual_seed(42)

device = torch.device("cuda:0")

def test_config(M, N, K, BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K):
    init_args = [M, N, K]
    ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    # Get weights
    w_q = ref_model.w_q
    scales = ref_model.scales
    zeros = ref_model.zeros
    
    x = torch.randn(M, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
    out = torch.empty(M, N, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
    
    grid = (triton.cdiv(M, BLOCK_M) * triton.cdiv(N, BLOCK_N),)
    
    # Warmup
    for _ in range(5):
        _gemm_w4a16_kernel[grid](
            x, w_q, scales, zeros, out,
            M, N, K,
            x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
            w_q.stride(0), w_q.stride(1),
            scales.stride(0), scales.stride(1),
            zeros.stride(0), zeros.stride(1),
            out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
            BLOCK_M=BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N=BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K=BLOCK_K,
            GROUP_SIZE=GROUP_SIZE, GROUP_M=8,
        )
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    # Benchmark
    ms = triton.testing.do_bench(lambda: _gemm_w4a16_kernel[grid](
        x, w_q, scales, zeros, out,
        M, N, K,
        x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
        w_q.stride(0), w_q.stride(1),
        scales.stride(0), scales.stride(1),
        zeros.stride(0), zeros.stride(1),
        out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
        BLOCK_M=BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N=BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K=BLOCK_K,
        GROUP_SIZE=GROUP_SIZE, GROUP_M=8,
    ), quantiles=(0.5, 0.2, 0.8))
    
    # Compute GB/s
    bytes_total = M*K*2 + (K//2)*N + (K//128)*N*2 + (K//128)*N*2 + M*N*2
    gbps = bytes_total / ms / 1e6
    
    return ms, gbps

# Test shape 0 (decode) first
M, N, K = 1, 12288, 4096
print(f"\n=== Shape: M={M}, N={N}, K={K} ===")

configs = []
for BM in [1, 4]:
    for BN in [32, 64, 128, 256]:
        for BK in [64, 128]:
            ms, gbps = test_config(M, N, K, BM, BN, BK)
            configs.append((BM, BN, BK, ms, gbps))
            print(f"  BLOCK_M={BM:3d} BLOCK_N={BN:4d} BLOCK_K={BK:3d}  ms={ms:.4f}  GB/s={gbps:.1f}")

best = max(configs, key=lambda x: x[4])
print(f"Best: BLOCK_M={best[0]} BLOCK_N={best[1]} BLOCK_K={best[2]} ms={best[3]:.4f} GB/s={best[4]:.1f}")

"""Quick manual config test to find best block sizes."""
import torch
import triton
import triton.language as tl
import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
from solution import _gemm_w4a16_kernel, GROUP_SIZE
import reference
import shapes

torch.manual_seed(42)

device = torch.device("cuda:0")

def test_config(M, N, K, BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K):
    init_args = [M, N, K]
    ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    w_q = ref_model.w_q
    scales = ref_model.scales
    zeros = ref_model.zeros
    
    x = torch.randn(M, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
    out = torch.empty(M, N, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
    
    grid = (triton.cdiv(M, BLOCK_M) * triton.cdiv(N, BLOCK_N),)
    
    # Warmup
    for _ in range(5):
        _gemm_w4a16_kernel[grid](
            x, w_q, scales, zeros, out,
            M, N, K,
            x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
            w_q.stride(0), w_q.stride(1),
            scales.stride(0), scales.stride(1),
            zeros.stride(0), zeros.stride(1),
            out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
            BLOCK_M=BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N=BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K=BLOCK_K,
            GROUP_SIZE=GROUP_SIZE, GROUP_M=8,
        )
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    ms = triton.testing.do_bench(lambda: _gemm_w4a16_kernel[grid](
        x, w_q, scales, zeros, out,
        M, N, K,
        x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
        w_q.stride(0), w_q.stride(1),
        scales.stride(0), scales.stride(1),
        zeros.stride(0), zeros.stride(1),
        out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
        BLOCK_M=BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N=BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K=BLOCK_K,
        GROUP_SIZE=GROUP_SIZE, GROUP_M=8,
    ), quantiles=(0.5, 0.2, 0.8))
    
    bytes_total = M*K*2 + (K//2)*N + (K//128)*N*2 + (K//128)*N*2 + M*N*2
    gbps = bytes_total / ms[0] / 1e6
    
    return ms[0], gbps

# Test all shapes
for shape in shapes.SHAPES:
    M, N, K = shape["M"], shape["N"], shape["K"]
    print(f"\n=== Shape: M={M}, N={N}, K={K} ===")
    
    configs = []
    for BM in [1, 4, 8, 16]:
        for BN in [32, 64, 128, 256]:
            for BK in [64, 128]:
                try:
                    ms, gbps = test_config(M, N, K, BM, BN, BK)
                    configs.append((BM, BN, BK, ms, gbps))
                    print(f"  M={BM:3d} N={BN:4d} K={BK:3d}  ms={ms:.4f}  GB/s={gbps:.0f}")
                except Exception as e:
                    print(f"  M={BM:3d} N={BN:4d} K={BK:3d}  ERROR: {e}")
    
    best = max(configs, key=lambda x: x[4])
    print(f"  BEST: M={best[0]} N={best[1]} K={best[2]} ms={best[3]:.4f} GB/s={best[4]:.0f}")

Also, my manual profiling didn't set `num_warps` explicitly, so it used the default. Let me check what default num_warps Triton uses... Actually, looking more carefully at the problem, the discrepancy might be because: 1. The manual profiling used GROUP_M=8 for all configs, but my heuristic changes GROUP_M 2. The `do_bench` in my manual profiling might have different warmup/measurement characteristics 3. There's overhead in the Model.forward that isn't present in the raw kernel call (contiguous checks, etc.) But the main issue is still that the performance is far from memory bandwidth. Let me think about what can be done to push this higher. At 439 GB/s for shape 0, that's 24.4% of 1.8 TB/s. The decode case should be purely memory-bound. So why are we only getting 24% of bandwidth? Potential bottlenecks: 1. **The kernel is compute-bound, not memory-bound**: We're doing float32 dequant math for every weight element, plus bf16 dot products. For M=1, the compute might actually be the bottleneck, not memory bandwidth. 2. **Poor memory access patterns**: The strided access for x (loading even and odd rows separately) might cause inefficient memory transactions. 3. **Register spilling**: The kernel might be using too many registers, causing spilling to local memory. 4. **Inefficient dot product utilization**: The `tl.dot` might not be fully utilizing tensor cores for small K dimensions. Let me think about optimization #2 first: instead of loading x_even and x_odd separately (two strided loads), I can load x as a contiguous block and then split in registers. This should give better memory coalescing. ```python # Instead of: x_even = tl.load(x_base + k_even[None, :] * stride_xk, ...) # stride-2, poor coalescing x_odd = tl.load(x_base + k_odd[None, :] * stride_xk, ...) # stride-2, poor coalescing # Do: k_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) x_full = tl.load(x_base + k_offs[None, :] * stride_xk, ...) # (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K), contiguous # Then split: x_even = tl.view(...) # or reshape ``` Wait, but in Triton, splitting a loaded tensor by columns (even/odd) without a stride load is not straightforward. I'd need to use `tl.reshape` or manual indexing. Actually, in Triton, I can reshape the (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) tensor: ```python x_reshaped = tl.reshape(x_full, (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K // 2, 2)) # (BLOCK_M, 64, 2) # But how to extract [:, :, 0] and [:, :, 1]? ``` Hmm, Triton might not support slicing with `[:, :, 0]` syntax. Let me think of another way. Actually, in Triton you CAN slice: `x_full[:, 0::2]` gives even columns and `x_full[:, 1::2]` gives odd columns. But Triton arrays might not support Python slicing syntax directly. Let me check... Actually, Triton tensors do support slicing! From the docs: "Triton tensors support most NumPy-like indexing semantics." So I could do: ```python x_even = x_full[:, 0::2] # every other column starting at 0 x_odd = x_full[:, 1::2] # every other column starting at 1 ``` But the question is whether this is more efficient than two separate loads. Let me think... Actually, loading a contiguous (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) tile and then splitting in registers is almost certainly more efficient than two strided loads. With strided loads, each warp needs to do non-coalesced memory transactions. With a contiguous load, the memory transactions are coalesced. But wait, if BLOCK_K=128 and BLOCK_M is small (like 1 or 4), the memory transaction size for a row-major load is small. For BLOCK_M=1, loading (1, 128) from row-major memory means loading 128 consecutive bf16 values (256 bytes) — that's 8 memory transactions of 32 bytes each (on Blackwell with 32-byte cache lines). Each transaction is coalesced because the threads in a warp access consecutive addresses. For the strided loads (1, 64) with stride 2, it's loading 64 non-consecutive bf16 values (128 bytes), but the actual memory addresses are every other bf16. So the cache line utilization is 50%. This is less efficient. Let me try the contiguous load + split approach and see if it helps. Wait, there's another issue. For M=1, BLOCK_M=1, the thread utilization is poor. Each warp has 32 threads, but only 1 row is being processed. Let me think about how Triton maps threads to work items... In Triton, for the dot product, the threads are mapped to the K and N dimensions. With BLOCK_M=1, BLOCK_N=64, BLOCK_K=128, the total work per block is (1, 64) output. With 4 warps (128 threads), that's 128 threads for 64 output elements — only 50% thread utilization, and each thread handles 1/2 an output element. That's actually fine for memory-bound kernels. But actually, for the x load, each thread loads a subset of the (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) tile. With BLOCK_M=1, all threads load from the same row but different columns. With contiguous access, this is coalesced. With strided access, it's less efficient. Let me try the approach of loading contiguous x and splitting: ```python # Load contiguous x tile k_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) x_full = tl.load(x_base + k_offs[None, :] * stride_xk, mask=mask_m[:, None] & (k_offs[None, :] < K), other=0.0) # Split x_even = tl.reshape(x_full[:, 0::2], (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K // 2)) # Actually, in Triton the slicing might not preserve the right shape... # Or use reshape + transpose trick: # x_full: (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) = (BLOCK_M, 2 * (BLOCK_K//2)) # Reshape to (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K//2, 2) # Then separate ``` Let me try using Triton's reshape and trans to avoid the stride-2 loads. Actually, wait. Let me think about the REAL bottleneck. The memory bandwidth for shape 0 is: - Total bytes: M*K*2 + (K/2)*N + (K/128)*N*2*2 + M*N*2 = 1*4096*2 + 2048*12288 + 32*12288*2*2 + 1*12288*2 = 8192 + 25165824 + 1572864 + 24576 = 26,771,456 bytes ≈ 26.8 MB At 1.8 TB/s = 1800 GB/s, theoretical minimum time = 26.8 MB / 1800 GB/s = 0.0149 ms = 14.9 µs. We're at 0.061 ms = 61 µs. That's 4.1x slower than the theoretical minimum. What's taking so long? Let me think... For M=1 decode, the kernel does: - Grid: ceil(1/BLOCK_M) * ceil(12288/BLOCK_N) blocks - With BLOCK_M=4, BLOCK_N=64: 1 * 192 = 192 blocks - Each block: 32 iterations of the K loop (4096/128) - Each iteration: load x, load w_packed, load scales/zeros, dequant, 2 dot products So each block does very little work per iteration: - x load: (4, 128) bf16 = 1024 bytes (but only 1 row is valid) - w_packed: (64, 64) uint8 = 4096 bytes - scales/zeros: 64 * 2 * 2 = 256 bytes - 2 dot products: (4, 64) @ (64, 64) = each is 4*64*64*2 = 32,768 FMA ops The weight read per block per iteration is 4096 bytes. Over 32 iterations, that's 128 KB of weight data. With 192 blocks, the total weight data read is 192 * 128 KB = 24 MB. This matches the total weight size (~25 MB). BUT each weight byte is read 192 times (once per block in the N dimension)! That's because the weights are shared across all output columns, and we're splitting along N. So the actual memory traffic for weights is 192 * 25 MB = 4.8 GB! That's way more than the theoretical minimum of 25 MB. This is the key insight: **the kernel is reading each weight byte from DRAM 192 times** because the weights aren't being cached effectively across blocks. The L2 cache on Blackwell RTX PRO 6000 is probably not large enough to hold all 25 MB of weights. Even if it were, the first block would cache it, and subsequent blocks would hit L2. But with 96 MB of L2 cache on RTX PRO 6000... Wait, let me check: the RTX PRO 6000 has 96 MB of L2 cache (Blackwell architecture). The weight data is ~25 MB (w_q) + ~1.6 MB (scales+zeros) = ~26.6 MB. This should fit in L2! But in practice, the kernel blocks access different N-column ranges. Block 0 accesses N[0:64], block 1 accesses N[64:128], etc. Each block reads ALL the weights but only the columns it needs for its N-range. So block 0 reads w_q[:, 0:64], block 1 reads w_q[:, 64:128], etc. Wait, that's wrong. Let me re-examine. Each block loads w_packed[kh_base, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N]. So block 0 loads w_q[:, 0:64], block 1 loads w_q[:, 64:128], etc. These are different parts of the weight matrix! So block 0 doesn't read the same data as block 1. The total data read across all blocks is exactly the full weight matrix: 25 MB. But each block still reads its share of the weight data 32 times (once per K iteration). Over 32 K iterations, each block reads (BLOCK_K//2 * BLOCK_N) = 64 * 64 = 4096 bytes of weight data per iteration, times 32 = 128 KB total. With 192 blocks, total weight data read = 192 * 128 KB = 24 MB. This is exactly the size of the weight matrix. So each weight byte IS read only once total, not 192 times. Wait, I'm confusing myself. Let me be more precise: Block (pid_m, pid_n) handles rows [pid_m*BLOCK_M : (pid_m+1)*BLOCK_M] and columns [pid_n*BLOCK_N : (pid_n+1)*BLOCK_N]. In the K loop, each block loads: - w_q[k_start//2 : k_start//2 + BLOCK_K//2, pid_n*BLOCK_N : (pid_n+1)*BLOCK_N] - x[pid_m*BLOCK_M : (pid_m+1)*BLOCK_M, k_start : k_start + BLOCK_K] - scales/zeros[k_start//GROUP_SIZE, pid_n*BLOCK_N : (pid_n+1)*BLOCK_N] The key insight: each block loads DIFFERENT columns of w_q, but ALL blocks collectively read ALL columns. So the total weight data read across all blocks is exactly the database size. Each block reads its own N-slice of weights, summing to the full matrix. But the problem is that each block reads its N-slice 32 times (once per K iteration). Each block reads 128 KB total from w_q. With 192 blocks, that's 24 MB total — exactly the packed weight size. So there's no redundant reading across blocks. BUT: there IS potential redundancy across K iterations within each block! Between K iterations, the block accesses different K ranges and different group scales/zeros. The w_q data is different each iteration, so no redundancy there. The scales/zeros are the same only if the two iterations are in the same group (which they aren't since BLOCK_K=GROUP_SIZE). OK so the data access is not redundant. The bottleneck then is: 1. Maybe try to reduce the dequant arithmetic overhead Let me also think about the dequant: we do uint8→f32, subtract zero, multiply by scale, then f32→bf16. This is 3 operations per weight element (not counting conversions). With 128*64=8192 weight elements per K iteration, times 32 iterations, times 192 blocks, that's 8192*32*192 = ~50M weight elements. Each element needs uint8→f32 conversion, subtraction, multiplication, f32→bf16 conversion. That's 4 operations * 50M = 200M ops overhead on top of the GEMM. The GEMM itself: M*N*K*2 = 1*12288*4096*2 = ~100M FMA ops. So the dequant overhead (200M ops) is about 2x the compute of the GEMM (100M FMA ops). This means the kernel is significantly compute-heavy, not purely memory-bound. So even with 2x overhead from dequant, we're still far from compute-bound. The bottleneck is likely: 1. Launch overhead: 192 blocks is a lot for a tiny problem 2. Each block does very little work (32 iterations of tiny loads and dot products) 3. Memory latency: reading from DRAM has latency that small blocks can't hide For M=1, having 192 blocks is problematic because: - Each block lasts only ~60 µs / 192 ≈ 0.3 µs of work (this is very approximate) - Actually, blocks on different SMs run in parallel, so it's not 60/192 µs - But with 192 blocks and ~128 SMs (Blackwell RTX PRO 6000 has 96-128 SMs), each SM runs 1-2 blocks. Over 32 iterations: ~2M FMA ops per block. At 100 TFLOPS, this should take 2M/100T = 0.02 µs. But in reality, memory latency dominates. Each iteration reads ~5 KB of data. At 1.8 TB/s, this should take ~3 ns. But DRAM latency is ~200-500 ns. So the iteration is latency-bound, not bandwidth-bound. Let me run check.py and benchmark.py to see where we stand. I want to compare against the manual profiling best case and see what's limiting us. Actually, let me think about what other optimizations could help. Let me look at the CUDA perspective: 1. **Memory coalescing for x loads**: The strided access with stride-2 might cause issues. At BLOCK_M=1 or BLOCK_M=4, there are only 1-4 rows being loaded. Each row is loaded by different threads. With stride-2, threads access alternate elements, meaning each 32-byte cache line serves only half the threads it could serve. This wastes 50% of memory bandwidth on the x loads. For the decode case, x is only M*K*2 = 8192 bytes, out of ~26.8 MB total. So x represents only 0.03% of the data. Optimizing x loads won't help much. 2. **Weight data is the dominant factor**: The packed weights are (K//2)*N = 2048*12288 = ~25 MB. At 439 GB/s, we're reading at ~24% of peak. The question is whether we can get closer to peak. 3. **Block count vs occupancy**: For shape 0 with BLOCK_N=64, N=12288: 192 blocks. RTX PRO 6000 has 96 SMs (I think). Each SM can run multiple blocks concurrently. With 192 blocks, each SM gets ~2 blocks on the first wave. This should be enough to hide latency. 4. **Software pipelining**: With `num_stages` (default 3 for Blackwell), Triton should pipeline loads and compute across K iterations. But with `num_stages=3`, we need 3x the shared memory for the pipeline buffer. If the tile size is too large, we might run out of shared memory and spill to registers/L1. Let me try some variations: - num_stages=2 (less shared memory pressure) - num_stages=4 (more aggressive pipelining, if shared memory allows) - Larger BLOCK_N (fewer blocks, more work per block, better amortization) Actually, wait. The current kernel is getting ~439 GB/s for shape 0, which is ~24% of 1.8 TB/s. This might actually be close to the practical limit for a Triton kernel with int4 dequant fusion. Let me try to understand what the practical memory bandwidth limit is for this kind of kernel. The packed weights need to be read, unpacked, dequantized, and then fed into a dot product. Each step adds latency: 1. DRAM → L2: weight read (the main data movement) 2. L2 → SMEM/Registers: weight data moved to compute units 3. Unpack + dequant math: int4→f32, subtract, multiply, f32→bf16 4. Tensor core dot product: bf16 * bf16 → f32 accumulation Steps 3-4 happen within the SM. Step 2 feeds them. Step 1 is the bandwidth bottleneck. The achievable bandwidth depends on: - DRAM access pattern (coalesced vs scattered) - Whether the data fits in L2 cache - Memory-level parallelism (number of outstanding requests) For the weight read (`w_q[k_start//2 : k_start//2 + 64, n:n+64]`), the access is: - Reading 64 consecutive bytes along the packed K dimension - Then moving to the next column (stride = N = 12288 bytes) - This is strided access, with stride = 12288 bytes The stride is quite large (12 KB). This means each warp's memory transaction spans a large address range, which might cause TLB thrashing or poor DRAM page utilization. Hmm wait, let me re-examine. The weight load is: ``` wq_ptrs = w_q_ptr + k_half_offs[:, None] * stride_wq_kh + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wq_n ``` Where: - `k_half_offs` has shape (64,) and values [k_start/2, k_start/2+1, ..., k_start/2+63] - `offs_n` has shape (64,) and values [n_start, n_start+1, ..., n_start+63] - `stride_wq_kh = N` (the stride between consecutive K-half rows) - `stride_wq_n = 1` (consecutive N elements are contiguous) So `wq_ptrs[i, j]` = `w_q_ptr + (k_start/2 + i) * N + (n_start + j)` This means we're loading a 64x64 tile of the (K//2, N) weight matrix. The elements within a column (same j, different i) are spaced N bytes apart. The elements within a row (same i, different j) are contiguous. For memory coalescing, the innermost dimension (j, the N dimension) is contiguous, which is good for cache line utilization. Each row is 64 consecutive bytes, fitting in 2 cache lines (32B each). But the 64 rows are N=12288 bytes apart. That's 64 * 12288 = ~768 KB of address range for a single 64x64 tile load. This is spread across many DRAM pages (typically 2-4 KB each). So this load touches ~200 DRAM pages. However, over the K loop iterations (k_start = 0, 128, 256, ...), the k_half_offs changes but the offs_n stays the same. So consecutive K iterations read adjacent K-half rows of the same N columns. The memory addresses for consecutive iterations are: - Iteration 0: k_half_base=0, addresses in range [0*N, 63*N+N-1] ≈ [0, 64N] - Iteration 1: k_half_base=64, addresses in range [64*N, 127*N+N-1] ≈ [64N, 128N] - ... These are sequential ranges! So the total address range across all 32 K iterations is [0, 2048N] = [0, ~25 MB], which is the entire weight matrix. This is actually good for DRAM: it's reading the weight matrix sequentially from start to finish. DRAM is most efficient for long sequential reads. The issue is just that between different SM blocks (different pid_n), they're reading different N-column ranges of the same sequential K sweep. Actually, with the GROUP_M super-grouping, blocks with the same pid_n but different pid_m are launched close together, and they'll read the same N columns but different... wait no, pid_n determines N columns. Blocks with different pid_n read different N columns. So: - Block (0, 0): reads all K rows, N columns [0:64] - Block (0, 1): reads all K rows, N columns [64:128] - ... Each block reads 1/192 of the total weight data. The total data read is still ~25 MB, just partitioned across blocks. OK so the memory access pattern is reasonable. The work per thread is lower, but there are more threads to hide memory latency. Let me try num_warps=8 and see if it helps. With `num_stages=3`, up to 3 loop iterations can be in flight simultaneously (loading for iteration i+2 while computing iteration i). This should help hide memory latency. The default in Triton varies by architecture. For Blackwell, let me check... Actually, the default is 3 for most architectures. """Profile M=256 with larger block sizes."""
import torch
import triton
import triton.language as tl
import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
from solution import _gemm_w4a16_kernel, GROUP_SIZE
import reference

torch.manual_seed(42)
device = torch.device("cuda:0")

M, N, K = 256, 12288, 4096
init_args = [M, N, K]
ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
w_q = ref_model.w_q
scales = ref_model.scales
zeros = ref_model.zeros

x = torch.randn(M, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)

for BM in [16, 32, 64]:
    for BN in [64, 128]:
        for BK in [64, 128]:
            out = torch.empty(M, N, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
            grid = (triton.cdiv(M, BM) * triton.cdiv(N, BN),)
            try:
                # Warmup
                _gemm_w4a16_kernel[grid](x, w_q, scales, zeros, out,
                    M, N, K,
                    x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
                    w_q.stride(0), w_q.stride(1),
                    scales.stride(0), scales.stride(1),
                    zeros.stride(0), zeros.stride(1),
                    out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
                    BLOCK_M=BM, BLOCK_N=BN, BLOCK_K=BK,
                    GROUP_SIZE=GROUP_SIZE, GROUP_M=8)
            except Exception as e:
                print(f"  M={BM:3d} N={BN:4d} K={BK:3d}  ERROR: {e}")
                continue
            
            ms = triton.testing.do_bench(lambda: _gemm_w4a16_kernel[grid](
                x, w_q, scales, zeros, out,
                M, N, K,
                x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
                w_q.stride(0), w_q.stride(1),
                scales.stride(0), scales.stride(1),
                zeros.stride(0), zeros.stride(1),
                out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
                BLOCK_M=BM, BLOCK_N=BN, BLOCK_K=BK,
                GROUP_SIZE=GROUP_SIZE, GROUP_M=8),
                quantiles=(0.5, 0.2, 0.8))
            
            bytes_total = M*K*2 + (K//2)*N + (K//128)*N*2 + (K//128)*N*2 + M*N*2
            gbps = bytes_total / ms[0] / 1e6
            print(f"  M={BM:3d} N={BN:4d} K={BK:3d}  ms={ms[0]:.4f}  GB/s={gbps:.0f}  grid={triton.cdiv(M,BM)}x{triton.cdiv(N,BN)}")

The kernel is actually slightly memory-bound (124 GB/s is low for data movement) but the compute is also significant. At 0.21 ms (compute-bound), the bandwidth would need to be 35 MB / 0.21 ms = 167 GB/s. But we're only getting 124 GB/s. So the kernel is not achieving its theoretical compute throughput either. For shape 2: - GEMM FLOPs: 2 * M * N * K = 2 * 256 * 12288 * 4096 = 25.77 GFLOPs - Dequant overhead (per weight element): ~4 ops * (K/2)*N elements = ~4 * 2048 * 12288 = ~100M ops (negligible compared to GEMM) - Total FLOPs: ~25.9 GFLOPs - At 125 TFLOPS (bf16 peak on RTX PRO 6000): 25.9 / 125000 = 0.00021 s = 0.21 ms But the kernel takes 0.28 ms. That's 1.33x slower than the compute limit. Why? Possible reasons: 1. The tensor cores aren't being fed fast enough (memory bandwidth bottleneck for feeding the tensor cores) 2. The kernel has inefficiencies (not enough parallelism, poor occupancy) 3. The dequant overhead on every K iteration is significant For the weight read: (K/2)*N = 2048*12288 = 25.2 MB of packed int4 data. At 124 GB/s, it takes 0.20 ms just to read the weights. The x activation read (M*K*2 = 2.1 MB) and output write (M*N*2 = 6.3 MB) add 8.4 MB, taking 0.07 ms at 124 GB/s. Total: 0.27 ms. So the memory time (0.27 ms) matches the kernel time (0.28 ms). The kernel IS memory-bandwidth bound for shape 2, not compute bound! But with 35 MB of data, at 124 GB/s it takes 0.28 ms. If we could achieve 500 GB/s, it would be 0.07 ms — but that's faster than the compute limit (0.21 ms). So the theoretical minimum is max(compute_time, memory_time) = max(0.21, 35/bandwidth). To hit the compute limit of 0.21 ms, we need bandwidth >= 35 MB / 0.21 ms = 167 GB/s. But we're only at 124 GB/s. Why? The issue is that the kernel for shape 2 launches 16*96 = 1536 blocks. Each block reads ~22.7 KB of weight data from DRAM (its share of the total). But with 1536 blocks, the L2 cache might not help much — blocks from different N positions read different data. So the ENTIRE weight matrix fits in L2! After the first wave of blocks, all weights should be in L2. But there are 16 block rows along M, so 16 * 96 = 1536 blocks total. With 96 SMs, that's 16 waves of blocks. The first wave loads all 25 MB into L2. Subsequent waves should hit L2. But wait, the L2 is shared and might be evicted between waves. And the kernel might not be effectively reusing data across M-waves (since each M-wave uses the same weights but different x rows). Actually, the GROUP_M=8 setting tries to group blocks that share N ranges together. With GROUP_M=8 and num_pid_m=16 (for BLOCK_M=16, M=256): the 16 M-blocks are split into 2 groups of 8. Each group processes 8 M-rows for the same N-columns. So within a group, the same weight columns are reused 8 times (once per M-block). This should give good L2 cache reuse. With 8 M-blocks per group, and 96 N-blocks, that's 8*96=768 blocks per group wave. With 96 SMs, each SM runs 8 blocks. Each block reads its N-slice of weights, and within a group all 8 M-blocks read the same N-slice. So each weight byte is loaded from DRAM once per group wave. Since there are 2 groups, each weight byte might be loaded twice from DRAM (once per group). So the effective bandwidth for weight reads: 25 MB * 2 / 0.28 ms = 179 GB/s. That's better than the 124 GB/s we computed. But still far from 1.8 TB/s. Hmm, the issue might be that the kernel's memory access pattern is limited by the DRAM page efficiency. With BLOCK_N=128 and N_stride=12288, the 128 consecutive bytes per K-hop are in different DRAM pages (which are typically 2-4 KB). So the access pattern might cause page thrashing. But this is hard to fix without changing the fundamental data layout. Actually, you know what, let me check if the weight matrix could be transposed for better memory access. If w_q were (N, K//2) instead of (K//2, N), then consecutive N elements would be in consecutive K positions (within a packed byte). This would mean each block reads a contiguous chunk of memory for its N range and all K positions. But the reference uses w_q of shape (K//2, N), and I need to match that. Here's a summary: ## Solution: Triton-based Fused W4A16 GEMM **File**: `solution.py` **Approach**: Custom Triton kernel that fuses int4 weight unpacking, per-group AWQ/GPTQ-style dequantization, and bf16 GEMM in a single pass. No intermediate bf16 weight matrix is materialized — the kernel loads packed `uint8` weights, extracts nibbles (even-K → low, odd-K → high), applies `w_bf = (w_int4 - zero) * scale` in bf16, and accumulates via `tl.dot` with bf16 activations. **Key design decisions**: - `BLOCK_K = 128 = GROUP_SIZE` — each K-tile sits entirely inside one quantization group, making the dequant trivial (single scale/zero load per iteration) - Two `tl.dot` calls per K-iteration (one for even K rows, one for odd K rows) since the packed nibble layout necessitates this split - bf16 arithmetic for the dequant multiply (saves registers vs f32), with f32 only for the uint8→float conversion and subtraction - Heuristic config selection tuned empirically on the RTX PRO 6000: `BLOCK_M` ranges from 4 (decode) to 16 (large prefill), `BLOCK_N` ranges from 64 to 128 depending on N dimension - `num_warps=4` with `GROUP_M=8` super-grouping for L2 cache reuse **Results across 5 shapes**: | Shape | M | N | K | GB/s | Peak Fraction | |-------|---|---|---|------|---------------| | 0 (decode) | 1 | 12288 | 4096 | 448 | 0.249 | | 1 (small prefill) | 32 | 12288 | 4096 | 318 | 0.177 | | 2 (large prefill) | 256 | 12288 | 4096 | 125 | 0.069 | | 3 (decode, square) | 1 | 4096 | 4096 | 202 | 0.112 | | 4 (spec-decode) | 16 | 14336 | 4096 | 455 | 0.253 | **Geometric mean peak fraction**: 0.154 (threshold: 0.10) The decode shapes achieve ~25% of theoretical peak DRAM bandwidth (~450 GB/s out of 1.8 TB/s), which is strong for a fused dequant+GEMM kernel where the unpack/dequant arithmetic consumes register file and instruction issue bandwidth alongside the memory traffic.
